    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="1511b4d5614e484d96948df1d02e854f">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'ad891926-8be5-4246-9430-c863c6633881'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d285303098" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Solvent molecule tuning is used to alter the redox potentials of solvents or ion-solvent binding energy for high-voltage or low-temperature electrolytes. Herein, an electrolyte design strategy that effectively suppresses solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) dissolution and passivates highly-reactive metallic Na anode via solvent molecule tuning is proposed. With rationally lengthened phosphate backbones with ─CH<sub>2</sub>─ units, the low-solvation tris(2-ethylhexyl) phosphate (TOP) molecule effectively weakens the solvation ability of carbonate-based electrolytes, reduces the free solvent ratio, and enables an anion-enriched primary Na<sup>+</sup> ion solvation sheath. The decreased free solvent and compact lower-solubility interphase established in this electrolyte prevent electrodes from continuous SEI dissolution and parasitic reactions at both room temperature (RT) and high temperature (HT). As a result, the Na/Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cell with the new electrolyte achieves impressive cycling stability of 95.7% capacity retention after 1800 cycles at 25 °C and 62.1% capacity retention after 700 cycles at 60&nbsp;°C. Moreover, the TOP&nbsp;molecule not only maintains the nonflammable feature of phosphate but also attains higher thermal stability, which endows the electrolyte with high safety and thermal stability. This design concept for electrolytes offers a promising path to long-cycling and high-safety&nbsp;sodium metal batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202304256-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202304256-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Limited resources and staggering consumption of lithium have raised cost concerns due to the mismatch between lithium supply and demand, impeding the boom of lithium-ion battery (LIBs) development for grid-level energy storage systems.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0001_R_d285303051e398" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Energy Nat. Commun. Adv. Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Praised for the cost-effectiveness and high natural abundance of Na resources, sodium-based batteries have attracted significant interest from both academia and industry, offering a sustainable alternative to meet the increasing energy storage demand.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0002_R_d285303051e403" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Science Adv. Mater. Nat. Rev. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among sodium-based batteries, sodium metal batteries (SMBs) stand out for the maximized energy density derived from the lowest redox potential (−2.71&nbsp;V vs. standard hydrogen electrode) and a high theoretical capacity (1165&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>) of metallic Na.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0003_R_d285303051e411" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the implementation of Na metal anode is hindered by short life-span and safety concerns since the high reactivity and dendrite issue of metallic Na, where side reactions between Na and liquid electrolytes are severe and the formed solid–electrolyte interphase (SEI) is hard to passivate Na.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0004_R_d285303051e416" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Soc. Rev. Chem">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, in the past several years, we have witnessed many strategies to stabilize metallic Na in terms of preloading Na in a matrix, electrolyte regulation, artificial SEI construction, and so on.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0005_R_d285303051e421" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Energy Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Eng. J.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>As another issue, the SEI created in sodium-based batteries is unstable due to the high solubility of Na–SEI components.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0006_R_d285303051e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Energy Lett.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Na–SEI dissolution rate was found to be higher than Li–SEI or K–SEI on carbon-based anodes in conventional carbonate electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0007_R_d285303051e433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Electrochem. Soc. J. Electrochem. Soc.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Due to the continuous SEI dissolution, sustained SEI breaking and reformation proceeded during battery operation, which further led to fast electrolyte consumption, low Coulombic efficiency (CE), and ultimately, the short lifetime of sodium-based batteries. To make it worse, high temperature (&gt;55 °C, HT) operation, an unavoidable scenario for stationary energy storage devices, breeds an accelerated dissolution rate, incurring more severe self-discharge and destroying the lifeline.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0008_R_d285303051e438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nat. Energy">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recently, electrolytes were saturated by typical SEI components such as NaF, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, and organic species in order to make the SEI species less soluble and mitigate the self-discharge, but the improvement was far from ideal.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0009_R_d285303051e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In pursuit of limited SEI dissolution, pioneering work reported by Zhang's group proposed a low-solvation electrolyte to minimize SEI dissolution on hard carbon anode by utilizing the low-polar fluorinated phosphate, where the electron-withdrawing fluorine atom in the solvents significantly lowered its binding with Na<sup>+</sup> cations.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0010_R_d285303051e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nat. Energy">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, designing new electrolyte recipes which can simultaneously suppress the SEI dissolution and stabilize the metallic Na, is urgently called for.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, tris(2-ethylhexyl) phosphate (TOP) with lengthened phosphate backbones by ─CH<sub>2</sub>─ groups are incorporated into conventional carbonate electrolytes (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202304256-fig-0001">1a</a>). An increased molecular volume endows the TOP solvent with lower solvation capability, which effectively decreases free solvents and weakens the solvation&nbsp;ability of the electrolyte, while massive free solvents in the high-solvation trimethyl phosphate (TMP)-based electrolyte are highly corrosive to Na metal (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0001">1b</a>). By reducing the unfavorable solvent attack and inducing a low solubility fluoride-rich interphase, this molecule-tuned electrolyte not only breeds the metallic Na compatibility but also enables a lower SEI dissolution rate at both room temperature (RT) and HT. The full cell using the as-developed electrolyte delivers a prolonged lifespan with restrained capacity decaying. Moreover, the fire-extinguishing TOP with its high boiling point endows the electrolyte with increased safety and thermal stability.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202304256-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0972f0ec-f43b-40d7-8d39-c83b8bf7604c/adma202304256-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0972f0ec-f43b-40d7-8d39-c83b8bf7604c/adma202304256-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/99df88a8-7af5-4d1a-94c4-849af157402c/adma202304256-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0972f0ec-f43b-40d7-8d39-c83b8bf7604c/adma202304256-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202304256-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202304256" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Design scheme and molecular structures of TMP and TOP solvents. b) Representative solvation structure of TMP-based and TOP-based electrolytes with the same volumetric ratio and salt concentration.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202304256-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202304256-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>A binary carbonate-based electrolyte consisting of 1.2&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> sodium bis(trifluoromethanesulfonate)imide (NaTFSI)/diethyl carbonate (DEC)-fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) (9:1 by vol, denoted as CBE) is first prepared due to desirable SEI-forming ability from the FEC decomposition and salt-dissolving ability from DEC. To target a nonflammable electrolyte, phosphates are mostly chosen because of their intrinsic nonflammability and strong solvation capability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0011_R_d285303051e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Energy Nat. Energy J. Am. Chem. Soc.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> By replacing 50 vol% of DEC with typical TMP, a phosphate-based electrolyte consisting of 1.2&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaTFSI/DEC-FEC-TMP (4:1:5 by vol) (denoted as PBE) is given and exhibits nonflammable feature (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Unfortunately, the Na/Na symmetric cell using the PBE affords a shorter lifetime&nbsp;of 120&nbsp;h in comparison with the conventional CBE-based cell (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information) due to the poor reduction stability of TMP.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0012_R_d285303051e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Chem. Commun.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, high polar TMP may accelerate the dissolution of SEI components and capacity loss due to its high solubility.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0010_R_d285303051e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nat. Energy">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To this end, we propose an electrolyte design concept to suppress SEI dissolution, as well as enhance&nbsp;battery safety via lengthening the alkyl chain of a commonly-used TMP to obtain TOP. As verified by the electrostatic potential (ESP) simulation, the lengthened alkyl group significantly reduces the electron cloud density of P═O, which would contribute to the weakened coordination with positive Na<sup>+</sup> ions (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202304256-fig-0002">2a</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0013_R_d285303051e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Energy Nat. Energy">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0002">2b</a>, progressive lengthening of alkyl groups to triethyl phosphate (TEP), tripropyl phosphate (TPP), tributyl phosphate (TBP), triamyl phosphate (TAP), trihexyl phosphate (THP), and TOP renders larger volume of individual molecules, which eventually reduce the molecular number from 8.55&nbsp;mol (TMP) to 2.12&nbsp;mol (TOP) in per liter of solvent. Noteworthily, the concentration of Na<sup>+</sup> solvation sites, such as P═O and C═O groups, simultaneously decreases from 8.55&nbsp;mol L<sup>−1</sup> (TMP) and 8.25&nbsp;mol L<sup>−1</sup> (DEC) to 2.12&nbsp;mol L<sup>−1</sup> (TOP), resulting in the weakened solvation ability of TOP (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), in agreement with the ESP simulation. Hence, by replacing the TMP with an equivalent volume of TOP, a unique molecule-tuned electrolyte formulation of 1.2&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaTFSI/DEC-FEC-TOP (4:1:5 by volume, denoted as MTE) is obtained. Due to the fire-extinguishing phosphate addition, MTE cannot be ignited according to the flammability test while CBE readily catches fire, indicating enhanced safety with the designed electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, thanks to the introduction of TOP with a high boiling point (215 °C), the weight loss of the electrolyte at 150 °C is reduced from 62.8% (CBE) and 44.4% (PBE) to 21.7% (MTE) according to the thermogravimetric (TG) analysis, suggestive of improved thermal stability (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Due to the low-solvation TOP, MTE is recorded with a moderate ionic conductivity of 1.01 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>, in comparison with CBE (2.60 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>) or PBE (5.50 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202304256-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/63187e24-f734-443d-ac31-3970c515cfb5/adma202304256-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/63187e24-f734-443d-ac31-3970c515cfb5/adma202304256-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f558b406-92a4-4708-b171-8cb901ae5b72/adma202304256-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/63187e24-f734-443d-ac31-3970c515cfb5/adma202304256-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202304256-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202304256" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Molecular structures of TMP and TOP solvents. b) Molar concentration of FEC and DEC, TMP, lengthened alkyl chain phosphate: TEP, TPP, TBP, TAP, THP, and TOP. c) Raman spectra of corresponding electrolyte systems: pure TMP solvent, pure DEC solvent, 1.2&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaTFSI/DEC-FEC-TMP (PBE), 1.2&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaTFSI/DEC-FEC (CBE), and 1.2&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaTFSI/DEC-FEC-TOP (MTE). d) Snapshots of the MD simulation box of the MTE. The RDF profile of e) PBE and f) MTE. g) Representative solvation cluster from the MD simulation of MTE. The Na, C, O, H, F, S, N, and P are marked with cyan, black, red, grey, pink, yellow, blue, and purple, respectively.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In probing the evolution of the solvation structure of Na<sup>+</sup> in electrolytes after molecule tuning, the Raman spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0002">2c</a>) is recorded. In the typical CBE system, a sharp peak locates at ≈902.1 cm<sup>−1</sup>, suggesting the dominant presence of free DEC solvent (C═O stretching frequencies).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0014_R_d285303051e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Phys. Chem. Lett.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the PBE system, peaks at ≈741.0 and ≈902.1 cm<sup>−1</sup> can be assigned to free TMP and free DEC. Free solvents in electrolytes are believed as the degradation origin of Na anode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0015_R_d285303051e623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Energy Lett.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, free solvents will assist SEI dissolution because they can provide binding sites for Na<sup>+</sup> ions in SEI components such as Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, NaF, or ROCO<sub>2</sub>Na.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0007_R_d285303051e637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Electrochem. Soc. J. Electrochem. Soc.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In comparison, the peak shift of the C═O stretching vibrations from ≈902.1 to ≈905.0 cm<sup>−1</sup> can be observed in the MTE, signifying that more DEC solvents are coordinated by Na<sup>+</sup> ions. Simultaneously, peaks assigned to the stretching vibrations of P═O are found to be relocated to a higher value of ≈744.5 cm<sup>−1</sup>. Consistently, the peak at ≈1278.6 cm<sup>−1</sup> is assigned to a P═O stretching bond of TMP and decreased in the MTE system. On the other hand, a new peak appears at ≈1304.6 cm<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). This peak is a higher shift of the P═O stretching bond, which clarifies the bonding between P═O moiety and Na<sup>+</sup>. These results suggest the reduced free solvents and establishment of more Na<sup>+</sup>/DEC and Na<sup>+</sup>/TOP coordination clusters in the designed MTE electrolyte by replacing TMP with TOP. The low-solvation TOP with lower solvation site concentration will solvate limited Na<sup>+</sup>; thus, most Na<sup>+</sup> tends to coordinate with carbonate solvents. Thereby, solvents with saturated binding sites and the reduced free solvents would alleviate the dissolution of the newly formed SEI components. To better visualize the coordination chemistry in the MTE, molecular dynamics (MD) simulations are further carried out (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0002">2d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7,S8</a>, Supporting Information). According to the radial distribution function (RDF) analysis, massive peaks of Na─O<sub>DEC</sub>, Na─O<sub>TMP</sub>, and Na─O<sub>TFSI-</sub> are located above 3 Å in the CBE or PBE, indicating most of DEC, TMP, and TFSI<sup>−</sup> are not involved in the first solvation sheath of Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0002">2e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7,S8</a>, Supporting Information). By comparison, peaks of the Na─O<sub>DEC</sub> and Na─O<sub>TMP</sub> pairs dramatically decline at above 3 Å in the designed MTE, signifying the decreased free TMP and DEC solvent molecules (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0002">2f</a>), consistent with the Raman spectra. Noteworthily, sharper peaks of Na─O<sub>TFSI−</sub> and Na─O<sub>FEC</sub> pairs at ≈2 Å reveal that incorporation of TOP possessing lower solvation ability allows more FEC and TFSI<sup>−</sup> anions to participate in the first solvation sheath of Na<sup>+</sup>, as visualized by the representative solvation clusters in MTE (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0002">2g</a>).</p>
         
         <p>To assess the dissolution of Na─SEI with different electrolytes, Na/Cu cell was assembled and capacity loss was quantified by applying the methodology established previously by Younesi's group.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0009_R_d285303051e713" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Concisely, the Na/Cu cells containing 150&nbsp;µL of electrolytes were cycled between 0.5&nbsp;mV and 2.0&nbsp;V with a low constant current (3.5&nbsp;µA cm<sup>−2</sup>). When cycling at low electrode potentials, the electrolyte composition underwent reduction at the working electrode (Cu), leading to the SEI formation on Cu. After every five cycles, a pause without current flow was applied for <i>x</i> h (<i>x</i> = 50, 30, 15, and 5). The discharge capacity increment (the difference between the capacity after and before the <i>x</i> h-long pause) clarified the parasitic reduction reaction on the Cu surface to compensate for the SEI dissolution during the open-circuit pause (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202304256-fig-0003">3a,b</a>) and could quantify the SEI dissolution in three electrolytes. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0003">3c</a>, the average capacity loss of baseline CBE was 12.63 µAh after 50&nbsp;h at RT while employing PBE led to a higher capacity loss (17.44 µAh) due to aggravated SEI dissolution in high polar TMP-based electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0003">3d</a>). Surprisingly, a much lower capacity loss of 6.89 µAh was recorded for the cell using MTE. When elevating the testing temperature to 60&nbsp;°C, the average capacity loss of all the corresponding cells exhibited increased capacity loss due to the accelerated SEI dissolution process. However, MTE still maintained a low average capacity loss of 8.84 µAh while the average capacity loss of baseline CBE (24.83 µAh) and PBE (36.42 µAh) reached 2.8 and 4.1 times compared to that of the MTE after 50&nbsp;h pause. Noteworthily, the SEI dissolution quantification test here was performed on the dense and flat Cu electrode with a low surface area. Nevertheless, the cycled Na surface usually presented as porous and would expose more surface to the electrolyte, and the SEI dissolution on the Na anode may be much more aggressive.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0016_R_d285303051e740" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Further, SEI was seen to continuously dissolve at the last 5 h pause using CBE and PBE electrolytes, 3.9 and 3.6 times larger than the average capacity loss of MTE-based cells (0.35 µAh). By fitting the capacity loss in these electrolytes at RT, the SEI dissolution rates were found to be ≈0.37, 0.28, and 0.13&nbsp;µA in CBE, PBE, and MTE, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0003">3e</a>). At HT, dissolution rates in CBE and PBE systems synchronously increased to 0.48 and 0.73&nbsp;µA, respectively. By contrast, employment of MTE afforded a self-discharge rate of 0.18&nbsp;µA, nearly four times smaller compared with PBE, indicating the suppressed SEI dissolution even at HT (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0003">3f</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202304256-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8980412d-4301-437b-988d-a842514e50f9/adma202304256-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8980412d-4301-437b-988d-a842514e50f9/adma202304256-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fed5edeb-7b4c-4d8a-a54e-8fff1b97e4d5/adma202304256-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8980412d-4301-437b-988d-a842514e50f9/adma202304256-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202304256-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202304256" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The discharge capacity during the reduction reaction of electrolyte decomposition before and after each pause at a) 25 °C and b) 60 °C. The average of three parallel cells is recorded as a solid line. Cell pause processes are marked by the grey area. The capacity loss after 50, 30, 15, or 5&nbsp;h pause in CBE, PBE, and MTE systems at c) 25 °C and d) 60 °C by representing three parallel cells with three shades of color. The capacity loss with fixed self-discharging rates as a function of the pause time at e) 25 °C and f) 60 °C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Besides the solvation capability of electrolytes, interphase composition, and structure also affected the SEI dissolution process due to the varied dissolving rate of different SEI components.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0017_R_d285303051e774" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence, XPS depth profiles were further carried out to grasp in-depth sight into the interphase chemistry on cycled Na electrodes. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202304256-fig-0004">4a</a> displays the outer SEI chemistry on Na electrodes retrieved from PBE after 50 cycles, in which organic components such as C─C, C═O, C─O, and C─H were recorded with intensive peaks accompanied by much fewer inorganics such as Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, and NaF. When etching to 40&nbsp;nm, organic species such as C═O still prevailed, alongside an increase in inorganic NaF from the FEC decomposition (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0004">4b</a>). From the element profile of the SEI, the C content remained high (14.2 at%) along with the 3.2 at% P as the sputtering proceeded to 40&nbsp;nm, indicating that the Na electrode was covered by the decomposition byproducts of TMP and carbonate (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0004">4f</a>). Similarly, the cycled Na electrode in CBE exhibited massive carbonaceous species, as derived from the violent DEC decomposition (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0004">4e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). Such organic build-up made the SEI too fragile and porous to guard the Na surface against electrolyte erosion, which would exacerbate both the dissolution and parasitic reactions on the SEI.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0018_R_d285303051e803" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In comparison, replacing the electrolyte with MTE rendered the SEI with more C─F species in the outer surface, coupled with much more NaF and S─F inorganics in the inner layer (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0004">4c,d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). Such variation could be attributed to more FEC/TFSI<sup>−</sup> involved in the interphase formation due to the more FEC and TFSI<sup>−</sup> anion involved in the first solvation sheath of Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0019_R_d285303051e821" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nat. Commun.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to the element distribution, both C and P contents featured an obvious decrease especially in the inner layer (6.8 at% of C and 0.3 at% of P at 40&nbsp;nm) (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202304256-fig-0005">5g</a>), in contrast to the value using PBE (15.4 at% C and 1.2 at% P) and CBE (23.1 at% C) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0005">5e,f</a>), which should originate from the suppressed carbonate and phosphate decomposition. Such an SEI enriched by inorganic NaF and salt-derived components can effectively accommodate volume change during Na deposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0020_R_d285303051e836" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Note that the solubility of NaF is much lower than that of organic substances such as ROCO<sub>2</sub>Na; therefore, the low-solubility NaF enrichment and lower solvation electrolyte collaboratively minimize the SEI dissolution on the Na electrode when employing MTE.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0009_R_d285303051e843" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">9</a>, <a href="#adma202304256-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0010_R_d285303051e843" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nat. Energy">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202304256-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/dba36fab-36c1-4e2f-b3c6-d9396309cdab/adma202304256-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/dba36fab-36c1-4e2f-b3c6-d9396309cdab/adma202304256-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/857c8114-6e39-44b2-b572-b85e84746cd6/adma202304256-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/dba36fab-36c1-4e2f-b3c6-d9396309cdab/adma202304256-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202304256-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202304256" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XPS depth profiles of cycled Na electrodes. C 1s, O 1s, and F 1s XPS spectra after 50 cycles in the a,b) PBE and c,d) MTE at 25 °C. SEI atomic ratios of the cycled Na electrodes in e) CBE, f) PBE, and g) MTE at 25 °C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202304256-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/517a6f0a-18c3-44fb-a6b5-ec0012077b94/adma202304256-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/517a6f0a-18c3-44fb-a6b5-ec0012077b94/adma202304256-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/85989726-c2fd-4ad3-8524-aa2cc717be47/adma202304256-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/517a6f0a-18c3-44fb-a6b5-ec0012077b94/adma202304256-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202304256-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202304256" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM images of plated Na with a,d) CBE, b,e) PBE, and c,f) MTE under 25 °C or 60 °C at the 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. The inset images show zoomed-in SEM of cycled Na. Cycling of Na electrode and CE of Na/Cu half cells using each electrolyte at g) 25 °C and h) 60 °C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Note that the morphology of the Na anode would exert influence on the SEI dissolution process by changing the contact area with electrolytes; scanning electron microscopy (SEM) was performed on the deposited Na.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0021_R_d285303051e893" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. J. Am. Chem. Soc. ChemSusChem">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to the SEM images of the Na electrode from the disassembled Na/Na symmetric cell using CBE or PBE, a dendritic and porous Na surface with overwhelming cracks can be seen (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0005">5a,b</a>). Such a porous surface will increase the contact of electrolyte/SEI and aggravate interphase dissolution.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0022_R_d285303051e901" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Sci. J. Am. Chem. Soc.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> MTE, in contrast, renders a dense Na layer with a few holes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0005">5c</a>), which can significantly lower the contact area between Na and electrolyte and effectively mitigate the SEI component dissolution. As temperature rises to 60&nbsp;°C, benefiting from the thermodynamically accelerated Na<sup>+</sup> mobility, Na deposits with larger radius are formed in both CBE and PBE but are still&nbsp;accompanied by excessive pores or cracking (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0005">5d,e</a>). By contrast, flat and larger Na grains are densely packed when paring with MTE (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0005">5f</a>), which can be attributed to the enhanced thermal stability of MTE.</p>
         
         <p>As the plating morphology is closely related to the electrochemical performance, the CE of Na/Cu cells was tested to evaluate the compatibility between these electrolytes and the Na anode. As displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0005">5g</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information, excellent reversibility with an average CE of 94.16% for 150 cycles and flat voltage curves were&nbsp;enabled by MTE, representing one of the best performances reported with phosphate-based electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0012_R_d285303051e927" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Chem. Commun.">12</a>, <a href="#adma202304256-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0023_R_d285303051e927" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ChemElectroChem">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In comparison, the cells cycled in CBE and PBE afforded an average CE of 76.12% and 73.29%, respectively, for less than 70 cycles accompanied by severe voltage fluctuations (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). The operation&nbsp;temperature was raised to 60&nbsp;°C for assessing the thermal compatibility between the Na anode and each electrolyte. With CBE or PBE applied, both cells experienced an early failure (less than 50 cycles) and a low CE of 72.72% and 59.17%, respectively, stemming from the accelerated parasitic reactions at HT (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0005">5h</a>). In comparison, the CE of the cell using MTE had been boosted to 93.95% for over 100 cycles, accompanied by restricted polarization (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). The long-time durability of Na electrodes in the three electrolytes was further compared by testing Na/Na symmetric cells. When operated at RT, the symmetric cell using the MTE rendered a prolonged life span for 600&nbsp;h at 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, in contrast to the short-lived operation in CBE (200&nbsp;h) or PBE (150&nbsp;h) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14a</a>, Supporting Information). Under a higher temperature (60&nbsp;°C), the cell using CBE or PBE underwent a rapid short circuit, while MTE still rendered long cycling for 400&nbsp;h, benefitting from both the enhanced thermal stability and Na-compatibility of MTE (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14b</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>To further assess the electrolyte compatibility under real operation scenarios, electrochemical performances of Na/Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cells were tested using these electrolytes. Cells with the conventional CBE delivered a capacity retention of 32.8% and an average CE of 99.31% after 600 cycles (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202304256-fig-0006">6a</a>). Moreover, rapidly increasing charge/discharge polarization could be observed due to the increased cell impedance(Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15,S16a</a>, Supporting Information), suggestive of the interfacial deterioration and high resistance buildup.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0024_R_d285303051e973" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater. Chem. Soc. Rev.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> By replacing the electrolyte with PBE, cyclability got worsened to 200 cycles along with low capacity retention (30.2%) and lower CE of 98.76%, stemming from the poor electrolyte/electrode interphase and dramatically-enlarged impedance in TMP-based electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16b</a>, Supporting Information). Surprisingly, with MTE employed, the capacity retention could be enhanced to 95.7% even after 1800 cycles, accompanied by a much higher average CE of 99.97%, well-restricted polarization, and cell impedance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0006">6b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16c</a>, Supporting Information). The results reveal the improved compatibility of MTE also toward the cathode side, perhaps stemming from the reduced free carbonate or phosphate solvents; thus, leading to the constrained solvent oxidation even after long-term cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0025_R_d285303051e988" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Chem Nat. Commun.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With increasing temperature from 25 °C to 60&nbsp;°C, the cells with the CBE or PBE showed rapidly enlarged voltage gap and decaying discharge capacity of 13.9% at 300 cycles and 27.3% after 139 cycles, respectively, which can be ascribed to the accelerated side reaction and electrode/electrolyte interphase dissolution at a higher temperature (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0006">6c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). Benefiting from the enhanced thermal compatibility and suppressed SEI dissolution, cells using MTE rendered a 700-cycle&nbsp;life-span with 62.1% of the capacity retained and a well-restrained voltage polarization (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0006">6d</a>), unveiling the reliability of the designed electrolyte even at HT. For further evaluating the practicality&nbsp;of our electrolyte design, Na/NVP full cells were assembled by pairing electroplated Na anode with high NVP loading cathodes (≈15&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>). As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information, full cells achieved stable cycling with 98.3% capacity retention over 200 cycles in MTE, while the capacity&nbsp;of control cells using CBE or PBE rapidly faded to 45.9% and 42.6%, respectively, in only 70 or 60 cycles.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202304256-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2cf8f264-5765-4697-a418-c5469f43e6c8/adma202304256-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2cf8f264-5765-4697-a418-c5469f43e6c8/adma202304256-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/75fd224b-877f-4b43-b4b9-0d3e608e7c60/adma202304256-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2cf8f264-5765-4697-a418-c5469f43e6c8/adma202304256-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202304256-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202304256" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Cycling performance of Na/NVP full cells and comparison on the surface chemistry of NVP cathodes. Long-term cycling stability of Na/NVP cells at a) room temperature and b) 60&nbsp;°C. Voltage curves using MTE at c) 25 °C and d) 60 °C. HRTEM images of the cycled NVP cathodes in e,f) PBE and g,h) MTE at 25 °C or 60 °C. i) Atomic ratios of O, P, V, Na, C, S, N, and F elements of the CEI derived from each electrolyte at 25 °C. XPS C1s, F1s, and S 2p spectra of the NVP cathodes in j) PBE and k) MTE at 25 °C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The NVP cathode was characterized by high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) and XPS after 20 cycles. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0006">6e,f</a>, the thickness of cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) formed in PBE reached ≈17&nbsp;nm at RT and ≈26&nbsp;nm at HT. Similarly, CBE rendered an accumulated CEI with ≈16 and ≈27&nbsp;nm thickness at RT and HT, respectively, due to the continuous oxidization of free solvents (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information). However, a compact and thinner CEI (≈4&nbsp;nm at RT and ≈13&nbsp;nm at HT) was formed in MTE (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0006">6g,h</a>), suggesting the suppressed CEI accumulation and superior cathode structural preservation. Based on the compositional analysis, a major difference in CEI formed in three electrolytes was the atomic ratio of the C element, which was almost halved by changing the electrolyte to MTE (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0006">6i</a>). The lower fraction of organic C-containing species such as C─H, C─C, C═O, and C─O groups found in MTE-derived CEI indicated the alleviated solvent oxidation,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0026_R_d285303051e1043" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> in comparison with the CBE or PBE-derived CEI (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0006">6j,k</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, dramatically increased S, N, and F elements could be observed at both RT and HT, in comparison with the CBE or PBE (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0006">6i</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information), indicating the facilitated TFSI<sup>−</sup> anion decomposition when applying MTE. We speculate this to be closely related to the participation of TFSI<sup>−</sup> in the first solvation sheath of Na<sup>+</sup>, and thereby, to its involvement in the interphase formation using MTE, while Na<sup>+</sup> ions in CBE or PBE were mainly solvated by corrosive solvent molecules.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0027_R_d285303051e1070" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Joule Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Accordingly, MTE-derived CEI afforded much sharper C─F and SO<i><sub>x</sub></i>F<i><sub>y</sub></i> peaks (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304256-fig-0006">6k</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information), which had been proved to effectively guard the cathode from continuous by-reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304256-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0010_R_d285303051e1088" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nat. Energy">10</a>, <a href="#adma202304256-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304256-bib-0028_R_d285303051e1088" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Joule">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202304256-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202304256-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, an advanced low-solvation electrolyte (1.2&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaTFSI/DEC-FEC-TOP) for nonflammable and high-stable SMBs has been successfully developed by tuning the molecular structure of phosphate. Specifically, the TOP molecule with its lower solvation sites concentration leads to higher anion content in the primary Na<sup>+</sup> solvation sheath and less free solvent, endowing the electrolyte with low-solvation capability and superior Na-compatibility. Low-solvation electrolyte and salt-derived interphase with lower solubility synergistically suppress the SEI dissolution in the as-design electrolyte. What's more, TOP possessing a high boiling point renders the electrolyte with improved thermal stability. Benefiting from the densely packed metallic Na deposition, a robust CEI layer, and the suppressed SEI dissolution, Na/NVP cells achieve impressive stability at both RT and HT. Electrolyte&nbsp;design via molecular tuning is expected to pave the way toward highly safe and reliable SMBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
